Compaction & GC

Last lesson we saw what a binlog looks like, and planted two "troublesome seeds": one, writes accumulate ever more small binlogs (each flush slices off a fragment, piling up shards); two, deletes only record tombstones, never truly erase (the void markers in delta_log keep stacking up). This lesson covers Milvus's two background janitors: compaction merges fragmented segments into larger, cleaner ones and physically applies tombstones; GC sweeps unclaimed orphan objects out of object storage. We'll see what each solves, the kinds of compaction, who schedules and who executes, and where the code lives in internal/datacoord.

📚 Analogy
Remember last lesson's library, "one bookshelf = a family of volumes"? After a while, two messes appear in the warehouse: one, a single shelf gets split into dozens of thin pamphlets (each archives just a little new stock), so reading one shelf means opening dozens; two, the cancellation list keeps growing, yet the struck-off books still sit on the shelf untouched. Compaction is the tidying clerk who comes periodically: he binds the thin pamphlets of one shelf into a few thick volumes, actually pulls out the void books per the cancellation list, and can even re-shelve by topic so similar books sit together — afterward, a reader opens fewer volumes and reads only valid books. GC is the warehouse's hauler: once the clerk finishes binding, the old thin pamphlets are unclaimed; the hauler waits past a set deadline, confirms no one uses them, then carts these orphan boxes off for destruction, freeing shelf space. Division of labor: the clerk makes reads fast, the hauler makes storage cheap.

Why compaction is needed: from fragments to tidiness

Compaction's motivation is exactly the two hooks planted at last lesson's end. First, small-file fragmentation: a segment flushes many times, producing a string of small binlogs by logID per field; and a collection has hundreds or thousands of segments. The more fragmented the files a query must open, the more metadata overhead, random I/O, and decode passes — especially fatal for object storage, which "fears small files." Second, tombstone buildup: a delete only appends "PK X voided at t" into delta_log, while the deleted row still sits in the insert binlog. As tombstones stack up, every row read must overlay-compare against the delete set — slow and memory-hungry. Compaction solves both at once: merge fragments + apply tombstones.

beforeseg A (small)seg B (small)seg C (small)+ a pile of delta tombstones
compactionmerge + physically delete void rows
afterseg D (large · clean · no tombstones)

The payoff after tidying is real read efficiency: fewer segments to open, each larger and more contiguous (friendlier sequential I/O), tombstones already cleared (no more overlay-compare), and data better-suited to indexing. In other words, compaction is the reconciler of Milvus's "write fast vs read fast" tension — writing only appends (fast but fragmented, with tombstones), leaving the slow work of "reshaping into a read-friendly form" for the background to do when the system is idle. This is the classic LSM-style idea: append fast, merge-and-tidy in the background.

It's worth thinking through one tradeoff: since compaction will eventually rewrite the data anyway, why not write large, tidy segments directly at write time, in one shot? The answer hides in the tradeoff between "write amplification" and "write latency." If writes had to keep data perfectly tidy, then every small batch arriving would have to read the existing big segment, merge with the new data, and rewrite the whole segment — one small write triggering one big rewrite, making write amplification explode and write latency unacceptable. Milvus's choice is to decouple the two: the write path does only the very fast thing of "appending small segments," guaranteeing low-latency, high-throughput writes; while "reshaping into big segments" is done once in the background, in batches, amortized, after enough has accumulated. The same rewrite cost, spread across thousands of writes, leaves each individual write almost unaffected. This is the essence of LSM-class systems: trade background batch-tidying for foreground write speed.

One more note on compaction's throttling philosophy: it is a background task that "saves where it can, defers where it may." DataCoord won't trigger immediately for a little fragmentation, but sets thresholds by segment size, small-file count, tombstone ratio, and so on, merging only when it's worthwhile; execution is also bounded by concurrency, lest background tidying steal resources from foreground reads and writes. This restraint of "don't disturb the main path" is a general rule for background maintenance — it serves reads and writes, but must never drag them down in return. Grasp this tradeoff and you understand why compaction always proceeds quietly "a beat behind" in the background, rather than racing to chase every single write.

Kinds of compaction: what each tidies

Compaction isn't a single thing. DataCoord, by different triggers, schedules different types of compaction, each doing one class of work. Their type enum is defined in proto's datapb.CompactionType:

typetriggertidying effect
MixCompactionone segment amassed too many small binlogs / fragmentsmerges fragmented binlogs into a larger, tidier segment (most common)
MergeCompactionseveral under-full small segments need joiningmerges several small segments into one bigger segment
Level0DeleteCompactionLevel-0 delete data (tombstones) accumulatedphysically applies L0 tombstones into the matching sealed segments
ClusteringCompactionre-organize by a clustering key for retrieval localityre-buckets data by clustering key, gathering similar data together
SortCompactionneed PK order to speed lookupssorts a segment's data by PK and rewrites it

Read this table and you'll see these compactions fall roughly into two camps: Mix and Merge are the "merge camp" — their core goal is stitching fragmented, scattered, small segments into large, tidy ones, applying ordinary tombstones along the way; this is the most frequently triggered class day to day. Clustering and Sort are the "reorder camp" — beyond merging, they actively adjust the physical order of data to boost read efficiency (see the next section); and Level0DeleteCompaction is a class of its own, dedicated to "actually landing hovering deletes onto segments." The key to understanding their distinctions isn't memorizing each name, but grasping one through-line: all compaction serves "reads" — by making segments fewer and tidier, clearing tombstones, or arranging data for better pruning.

The one worth spelling out is Level0DeleteCompaction, the second half of last lesson's "deletes don't truly delete." A delete message is first buffered as Level-0 delete data — a batch of deltalogs holding only "void PK + timestamp," not yet landed on any specific sealed segment, like a layer of "pending deletes" hovering above all segments. Level0DeleteCompaction is exactly what sinks and applies this L0 tombstone layer into the sealed segments they should act on, making the deleted rows physically vanish. DataCoord keeps an L0 delete view (LevelZeroCompactionView in internal/datacoord/compaction_l0_view.go) to track "which L0 tombstones should merge with which segments." The full visibility mechanism of deletes is dissected next lesson.

Level0DeleteCompaction: sink the hovering L0 tombstones, apply them into sealed segments
L0 tombstonespk=102@t9 · pk=205@t9
↓ applyroute to segments holding the pk
segment (after)rows 102/205 physically deleted

Who schedules, who executes

Before getting to the division of labor, let's fully explain ClusteringCompaction and SortCompaction — the two kinds that "don't just merge but also reorder" — because they directly serve faster reads. Ordinary Mix/Merge only stitch fragments larger and apply tombstones, without changing the data's arrangement; whereas Clustering and Sort actively reorganize the physical order of data within segments, letting later queries skip more irrelevant data.

ClusteringCompaction (reorganize by clustering key) works like this: give the collection a clustering key (say "region" or "category"), and at compaction time re-bucket data by that key, gathering "same-class" data into neighboring segments. Then a filtered query (e.g. "only East China") can, by each segment's min/max stats (recall last lesson's stats binlog), skip whole segments of irrelevant buckets and scan only the few that truly match — this is the power of "data locality" in databases: place data frequently queried together physically together, and the query's pruning rate rises. SortCompaction (sort by primary key) rewrites a segment's data ordered by PK, making PK point lookups, range scans, and dedup more efficient; sorted data also tightens min/max ranges and eases bloom checks. Both share a trait: at the cost of one background rewrite, they pre-shape data into a read-friendly form, amortizing the cost from "every query" forward into "one tidying pass."

Compaction's division of labor again proves the refrain "coordinators schedule, nodes execute" (Lesson 9). DataCoord is the brain: it scans segment metadata, by various compaction policies (compaction_policy_*) decides "which segments to merge and which compaction to run," generates compaction tasks, enqueues and dispatches them. The one doing the heavy lifting — reading old binlogs, merging, applying tombstones, writing new binlogs — is the compaction worker on a datanode. This is precisely Lesson 17's point that "DataNode's main present role is compaction": streaming writes went to StreamingNode, and DataNode steps back to focus on this background tidying.

DataCoord
scan meta · pick policy · generate tasks
→
compaction queue
prioritize · dispatch
→
datanode worker
read old binlogs · merge · apply tombstones · write new binlogs
→
write back meta
new segment online · old segments marked dropped

On completion comes a key act: the worker writes new segments, and DataCoord atomically switches metadata from "the old small segments" to "the new big segment," bringing the new one online for queries and marking the old ones dropped. This step must be atomic, and that matters greatly: it guarantees any query, at any instant, sees either the whole batch of old segments or the one new segment — never an intermediate state where "old and new are both visible and a row gets counted twice" or "the old is withdrawn, the new not yet online, and data vanishes into thin air." It is precisely this "atomic metadata switch" guarantee that lets compaction — a major operation that "quietly swaps out data in the background" — be completely transparent and imperceptible to foreground queries. Note — at this moment the old segments' binlogs still sit in object storage, undeleted; they are merely no longer referenced by metadata. Truly sweeping away these "dropped, unreferenced" physical files is the job of the other janitor, GC.

pkg/proto/datapb/data_coord.pb.goCompactionType: the compaction-type enum (excerpt)
// generated from milvus-proto/go-api; datacoord schedules by these
const (
    CompactionType_MergeCompaction         CompactionType = 2
    CompactionType_MixCompaction           CompactionType = 3
    CompactionType_Level0DeleteCompaction  CompactionType = 7 // apply L0 tombstones
    CompactionType_ClusteringCompaction    CompactionType = 8 // reorganize by clustering key
    CompactionType_SortCompaction          CompactionType = 9 // sort by primary key
)

GC: sweeping away ownerless orphan objects

Compaction produces a batch of old binlogs that are "logically discarded, physically still present"; dropping a collection, partition, or segment also leaves many no-longer-referenced objects. GC (garbage collector) is the background loop responsible for reclaiming these orphan objects, with code in internal/datacoord/garbage_collector.go. It periodically scans the binlogs in object storage, checks against metadata "is this object still referenced by any live segment," and for those of dropped segments or orphan files no one claims, deletes them from object storage.

1

scan and compare

recycleUnusedBinlogFiles

enumerate binlogs in object storage, find orphans "referenced by no live segment" against metadata.

2

handle dropped segments

recycleDroppedSegments

for segments marked dropped, reclaim all their binlogs (insert/delta/stats).

3

delete only past TTL

delete only objects past the retention period (TTL), leaving a safe window for in-flight reads and rollback.

4

physical delete

batch-delete by object-storage prefix, truly freeing space.

The most crucial safety valve in GC's design is the TTL (retention period): it never deletes an object the instant it becomes an orphan. The reason is the many "time skews" in a distributed system — some QueryNode may still be reading an old segment just replaced by compaction, some operation may need to roll back. Deleting only objects "orphaned beyond TTL" gives every in-flight reference a safe window: better to let garbage linger a while than to ever mis-delete data still in use. This echoes last lesson's point: object storage's prefixed, enumerable layout lets GC efficiently enumerate and clean by segment or collection prefix.

It's worth pausing to ask: where do "orphans" in object storage actually come from? Their sources are more numerous than you'd think. Old segments replaced by compaction are the biggest class — one merge batch-creates a pile of "dropped, awaiting reclaim" old binlogs; DDL that drops a collection / partition / segment makes swaths of objects instantly lose their references; failed or timed-out flush, compaction, or index-build tasks may break off mid-write, leaving half-finished orphans that "wrote files but never registered into metadata"; even a metadata rollback can turn some already-persisted objects ownerless. GC's decision logic therefore can't just look at "does the file exist," but must take metadata as the yardstick and check in reverse: anything present in object storage but with no reference found in live-segment metadata is an orphan candidate. This is the classic reclaim mindset of "authoritative metadata is truth, physical storage aligns to it" — only when metadata says it's no longer needed may the physical file be cleaned.

A common misconception needs clearing up here too: a delete does not immediately make data vanish from object storage, and even a DDL "drop segment" only first marks the segment dropped. The real release of physical space is always done asynchronously by GC, past TTL. So if you delete a batch of data and notice object-storage usage doesn't drop right away, that's not a bug but the normal behavior of this "separate logical voiding from physical reclaim" design — it trades a little storage delay for deletes that are low-latency, rollback-able, and friendly to in-flight reads. Grasp this, and you grasp the same wisdom recurring across Milvus's whole write path: decouple "fast bookkeeping" from "slow settling."

internal/datacoord/garbage_collector.gogarbageCollector: reclaim orphans and dropped segments (excerpt)
type garbageCollector struct {
    meta    *meta
    handler Handler
    option  GcOption // holds TTL and retention policy
}

// background loop: clean unreferenced binlogs and dropped segments
func (gc *garbageCollector) recycleUnusedBinlogFiles(ctx context.Context) { /* enumerate · compare · past-TTL · delete */ }
func (gc *garbageCollector) recycleDroppedSegments(ctx context.Context, signal <-chan gcCmd) { /* reclaim all binlogs of dropped segments */ }

Seen together, compaction and GC are a relay, one after the other: compaction rewrites "fragmented, dirty" data into "tidy, clean" new segments and voids the old ones logically; GC, past TTL, sweeps these old segments away physically. The former optimizes reads (fewer, cleaner segments), the latter optimizes storage (don't keep paying for discarded data). Both are scheduled by DataCoord — the silent yet vital self-maintenance of Milvus's write path "after the write"; without them, the system slowly chokes on small files and tombstones, queries grow ever slower and storage ever more bloated. These two background janitors, though never on the main stage of writes and queries, are the behind-the-scenes bedrock that lets Milvus run stably over the long haul. Next lesson, we formally crack open "delete and upsert," seeing how tombstones, bloom filters, and the MVCC timestamp together decide "whether a row is visible at all."

🌍 Big picture
In one line: writing only appends fast, leaving two "tech debts" — small-file fragments and delete tombstones — repaid by two background janitors. Compaction (scheduled by DataCoord, executed by a datanode worker) merges fragments and physically applies L0 tombstones (datapb.CompactionType_Level0DeleteCompaction), and can reorganize by clustering key (Clustering) or sort by PK (Sort); GC (garbage_collector.go), past TTL, sweeps away objects of orphans and dropped segments. The former speeds reads, the latter saves storage.
📌 Key points
  • Two tech debts: small-file fragments from repeated flushes + tombstone-only deletes, repaid by compaction and GC.
  • Compaction types: MixCompaction/MergeCompaction (merge fragments), Level0DeleteCompaction (physically apply L0 tombstones), ClusteringCompaction (reorganize by clustering key), SortCompaction (sort by PK).
  • Schedule vs execute: DataCoord picks policy and generates tasks; a datanode worker reads old binlogs, merges, writes new segments (echoes Lesson 17).
  • Level-0 deletes: a delete is first buffered as an L0 tombstone, tracked by compaction_l0_view.go, and only Level0DeleteCompaction applies it physically into segments (expanded in Lesson 20).
  • GC and TTL: garbage_collector.go reclaims orphan / dropped-segment objects, deleting only those past TTL, leaving a safe window for in-flight references.
  • Division of labor: compaction optimizes reads, GC optimizes storage; both scheduled by DataCoord.

🧪 Self-test - think about the design

1. Regarding the scheduling and execution of compaction, which statement matches Milvus's current architecture?
  1. DataCoord scans segment metadata and, by policy, generates and dispatches compaction tasks; the one actually reading old binlogs, merging, and writing new segments is the compaction worker on a datanode
  2. Compaction is done synchronously by the Proxy on the write path
  3. Compaction must be executed by a dedicated indexnode
  4. Compaction is merged on the fly by QueryNode at query time

💭 Open questions (no single right answer - just think or try)

Delete & upsert

"Delete a row" sounds like the most trivial operation in a database, yet on an append-only, immutable storage substrate it hides a twist: once a binlog is written it can't be changed — so what does "delete" actually delete? This lesson fully cracks open delete and upsert — how a delete becomes a delete message in the WAL, then settles into Level-0 (L0) delete data / deltalogs; how bloom filters route a delete to the segments that might hold the PK; why upsert is just "delete + insert"; and the key that unifies it all — timestamp-based MVCC (multi-version concurrency control): whether a row is "visible at all" is decided jointly by the read's guarantee timestamp and each version's timestamp.

📚 Analogy
Picture a collection as a hotel's guest registry — and one that can only be appended to, never altered. When a guest checks out, the front desk does not flip back and strike out that line (the ledger can't be changed); instead it stamps on a new page: "guest X checked out at time t" — that is a delete tombstone. To know "which page a guest is on," the desk needn't read the whole ledger but checks a quick index card (bloom filter): it tells you only "this ledger definitely lacks this guest" or "maybe has them, go look" — to quickly rule out impossible ledgers. A guest wants to change rooms (update info)? The way is "stamp checkout on the old room + write a check-in for the new" — that is exactly upsert = delete + insert. And the question "who is actually staying now" depends on which moment you read the ledger as-of: read the "as-of-now" snapshot to cleanly count current guests — that is timestamp-based MVCC.

A delete's journey: message → WAL → L0 deltalog

Recall Lessons 15 and 16: a write doesn't go straight to storage but first becomes a message Appended into the WAL, and the WAL is the single source of truth. A delete takes the same road — it doesn't go to object storage to "find that row and erase it," but generates a delete message carrying the PK to void and a delete timestamp, Appended into the WAL, queued into the same global time order as inserts.

1

client issues delete

specify "whom to delete" by PK or expression.

2

Proxy stamps a timestamp

asks TSO for a delete ts, fixing this delete's place in the global order.

3

Append to WAL

the delete message (PK + ts) is written to the WAL, in the same stream as inserts.

4

settle as L0 deltalog

the consumer buffers the delete as Level-0 delete data (deltalog), not yet landed on a specific sealed segment.

5

Level0DeleteCompaction applies it

the background sinks and applies the L0 tombstone into the matching segments (Lesson 19).

The key insight here is: a delete is modeled as a timestamped, immutable event, not an "in-place modification." It first exists as Level-0 delete data — a batch of deltalogs holding only "void PK + delete timestamp," hovering above all sealed segments, not yet applied to any specific one. Why hover first? Because "which segments this delete should act on" needs per-segment judgment (next section), and the rewrite cost of truly erasing rows from data is large — so, like inserts, deletes follow "book fast, settle in the background": record the tombstone quickly into L0, leaving the slow "physical apply" to Lesson 19's Level0DeleteCompaction. This is the full landing, on the write side, of last lesson's "deletes don't truly delete."

There's also an easily-overlooked yet crucial design here: deletes and inserts share the same WAL, queued in the same global time order. Why does this matter so much? Because only when "insert this row" and "delete this row" are placed on the same monotonically-increasing time axis can the system unambiguously decide their ordering — is it "insert first, delete later" (then the row should vanish), or "delete first, then a new row of the same PK inserted later" (then the new row should stay)? Imagine if deletes took a separate channel: the two streams' orderings would need extra coordination to align, and the slightest slip would produce bizarre results like "deleted yet alive, or alive yet wrongly deleted." Folding deletes into the same WAL as inserts lets the TSO, that "global number-issuer" (Lesson 16), adjudicate the ordering of all write events together, dissolving delete/insert ordering conflicts at the source. This is the natural extension, to delete semantics, of Milvus's creed "log as data, WAL as the single source of truth."

Routing a delete with bloom filters

A delete message says "void PK X," but row X may sit in one of hundreds or thousands of segments — you can't blindly stuff this tombstone into every segment. This is where the bloom filter comes in (recall Lesson 18: it's serialized into the stats binlog alongside PK stats). The system takes PK X and asks each segment's bloom: "could this segment possibly hold X?" — the bloom answers only "definitely not" or "maybe." Every segment answering "definitely not" is safely skipped, and only the few answering "maybe" need the tombstone applied.

Bloom routing: screen each segment with the to-delete PK X, land the tombstone only on segments that "might hold X"
seg1bloom: definitely not → skip
seg2bloom: maybe → apply tombstone
seg3bloom: definitely not → skip
seg4bloom: maybe → apply tombstone

The bloom filter's "never a false negative" property is the lifeline of correctness here: if it says "definitely not," it truly isn't, so a skipped segment never wrongly misses a row that should be deleted; while its occasional "false positive" (says maybe, a closer look finds nothing) at most causes a little wasted work, never a wrong delete. This asymmetric guarantee of "rather over-check than ever miss" is exactly the foundation on which delete routing can confidently prune. In Milvus this bloom filter is held by PrimaryKeyStats (internal/storage/stats.go) — it carries the PK bounds (MaxPk/MinPk) and that BF (bloomfilter.BloomFilterInterface), exposed via BloomFilterSet/pkoracle, the decision basis for "which segments a delete should land on."

It's worth appreciating bloom routing's significance at scale: a large collection may have thousands of segments, and if every delete stuffed its tombstone indiscriminately into each one, the cost of a delete would balloon linearly with the segment count, with the vast majority of tombstones landing on segments that "don't hold the PK at all" — pure waste. The bloom filter compresses this from "broadcast to all segments" down to "precise delivery to a few candidate segments" — at the price of one tiny probabilistic bitmap per segment, it greatly shrinks the delete write volume and leaves far fewer tombstones for later Level0DeleteCompaction to process. This is the classic engineering tradeoff of "spend a little summary space to save a lot of blind I/O," two sides of the same mechanism as Lesson 18's bloom helping queries "skip whole segments": queries use it to skip irrelevant segments, deletes use it to deliver only to relevant ones.

internal/storage/stats.goPrimaryKeyStats: a segment-level PK summary, with bloom filter (excerpt)
type PrimaryKeyStats struct {
    PkType   int64                            // the PK type
    MaxPk, MinPk PrimaryKey                   // PK upper/lower bounds
    BF       bloomfilter.BloomFilterInterface // bloom: routes deletes to candidate segments
}

upsert = delete + insert

Once you see that a delete is "append a timestamped tombstone" and an insert is "append a timestamped new row," upsert becomes obvious: it is not "change the old value to the new in place," but is split into two steps — for the same PK, delete first, then insert. The old version is voided by a tombstone, and the new version is written as a row with a later timestamp; the same PK thus physically holds both old and new versions at once, distinguished by timestamp. This explains the true meaning of last lesson's line "upsert = delete + insert (same PK, new version)."

upsert PK 102: the old version is voided by a tombstone, the new written with a later ts (both coexist, latest-ts wins)
old versionpk=102 · v0 · inserted@t3
tombstonepk=102 · voided@t8
new versionpk=102 · v1 · inserted@t8

Seeing upsert as "delete + insert" has a very practical benefit: the whole write path needs no special mechanism just for upsert. Whatever delete has (tombstones, bloom routing, L0, compaction apply), upsert reuses directly; whatever insert has (columnar binlogs, ordering by ts), upsert reuses too. A seemingly standalone "update" semantic is elegantly reduced to a composition of two existing primitives — a model example of "building rich semantics from a few orthogonal primitives" in system design. The cost is that a PK briefly holds multiple versions, but that is exactly caught by the MVCC below: multiple versions are MVCC's bread and butter.

This "delete + insert" reduction also incidentally explains a semantic detail of upsert: it does not guarantee field-level partial updates. Because the new version is rewritten as a whole row, if you upsert with only some fields, the omitted fields are wholesale overwritten by defaults or rewrite rules, not preserved as SQL's UPDATE ... SET col=... would by touching one column and keeping the rest. Understand that "upsert is essentially a whole-row replacement (void the old row + write a new row)," and you won't mistakenly assume it can "change one field in place" — a common cognitive gap between vector DBs and traditional row stores on update semantics. The root cause is again that iron law: the underlying binlog is immutable, so any "change" can only be expressed as "void the old, append the new" — there is no true in-place modification.

A frequently-asked detail: since upsert is "delete then insert," what happens when the same PK is upserted many times over? The answer — each time appends a "tombstone + new version" pair, so a PK accumulates a string of versions laid out by timestamp, but any read at any moment only hits the latest non-voided version (adjudicated by the next section's MVCC rule). Those earlier old versions are neither read nor occupy space forever — they are exactly the targets of compaction from last lesson and Lesson 19: when tombstones are physically applied and segments merged, the voided old versions are truly erased. So even "the historical baggage left by repeated upserts" is backstopped and reclaimed by background compaction, and the write side still only appends fast without worrying about cleanup — once more proving the through-line spanning the whole write path: fast bookkeeping in the foreground, slow settling in the background.

Unifying it all: timestamp-based MVCC

By now, insert, delete, and upsert have all been reduced to "a pile of timestamped immutable events": an insert is "a row born at ts," a delete is "a PK voided at ts." So how is "whether a row is visible right now" decided? The answer is the core rule of MVCC (multi-version concurrency control) — everything is judged by timestamp. MVCC doesn't "remove" or "overwrite" anything; it keeps all versions on the time axis and hands the question of "which version counts" to the timestamp a read carries.

A read carries a guarantee timestamp T (guarantee-ts, determined by the consistency level, expanded in Lesson 30). A row is visible to this read if and only if two conditions both hold:

MVCC visibility rule: read at guarantee-ts T, a row is visible ⇔ already inserted AND not voided by an earlier tombstone
condition 1the row's insert ts ≤ T (born before T)
andAND
condition 2no tombstone with ts ≤ T voids it (not deleted as of T)

Apply this rule to the earlier example and it's crystal clear. For PK 102: reading at T=t5, you see v0 (inserted@t3 ≤ t5, while voided@t8 is still in the future, not yet in effect); reading at T=t9, v0 is voided by the t8 tombstone and invisible, so you see v1 (inserted@t8 ≤ t9). The same underlying data, with a different read timestamp T, carves out a different point-in-time snapshot — that is the power of "multi-version": writers just keep appending new versions and tombstones, while a reader, with one T, cleanly carves out a consistent "as-of-now" view, and reads and writes never block each other.

This concurrency model of "reads take a snapshot, writes append" yields a property extremely precious in a distributed vector DB: reads and writes never wait on or lock each other. In a traditional database, "modifying while reading" often means lock contention, reads blocked by writes; under MVCC, the new versions and tombstones being continuously written simply don't disturb a query running at an earlier T — that query always sees the consistent snapshot corresponding to its departure moment T, even if the underlying data has since been changed beyond recognition. For a vector DB needing high-throughput writes yet stable low-latency retrieval, this is vital: massive inserts, deletes, and upserts keep appending to the tail of the time axis, while every search grabs a T to cleanly "freeze" a motionless world to retrieve over — the two run on separate tracks, never dragging each other down. This is what the words "multi-version" truly buy — not to preserve history, but to decouple reads and writes along the time axis.

events of PK 102insert v0 @t3tombstone void @t8insert v1 @t8
read T=t5see v0 (t8 is future)
read T=t9see v1 (v0 voided)

This key of "timestamp decides visibility" is the very foundation by which Milvus reconciles consistency and high concurrency in a distributed setting. It needs no read/write locks on the data; instead it lets "reads" and "writes" each advance along the time axis: writes only append, a read takes a snapshot. But it leaves one question unanswered: how exactly is that guarantee timestamp T set? How long must reading "the latest" wait to guarantee seeing everything? That is the stage of consistency levels (Strong / Bounded / Eventual, etc.) — they trade off "how fresh you see" against "how long you wait." The full mechanism — how TSO issues numbers, how the delegator waits on watermarks, how snapshots align — is reserved for Lesson 30. For this lesson, firmly remember the unifying rule: an insert stamps a row's birthday, a delete stamps its deathday; a read holding T sees only rows whose "birthday ≤ T and deathday > T or none." Hold this rule firmly and you grasp the single yardstick spanning all three write operations — insert, delete, and upsert.

🌍 Big picture
In one line: on append-only storage, a delete is not an erase but stamping a timestamped tombstone — it enters the WAL as a delete message, settles into an L0 deltalog, is routed by bloom filters (PrimaryKeyStats's BF, internal/storage/stats.go) to segments that might hold the PK, and is finally physically applied by Level0DeleteCompaction (Lesson 19). upsert = delete + insert (same PK, a new version with later ts). Unifying it all is timestamp-based MVCC: read at guarantee-ts T, a row is visible ⇔ insert ts ≤ T and no tombstone with ts ≤ T voids it; how T is set is decided by the consistency level (Lesson 30).
📌 Key points
  • Delete is a tombstone: a delete becomes a WAL delete message (PK + ts), settling into L0 delete data / deltalogs, with no in-place erase.
  • Bloom routing: ask each segment's bloom with the to-delete PK (PrimaryKeyStats.BF, internal/storage/stats.go); "definitely not" segments are skipped, the tombstone lands only on "maybe" segments; no false negatives guarantees no wrong delete.
  • Physical apply: L0 tombstones are truly sunk and applied into segments by Level0DeleteCompaction (Lesson 19); the write side only books fast.
  • upsert = delete + insert: for the same PK, delete then insert, old and new versions distinguished by ts; reuses all delete and insert machinery, no special path.
  • Timestamp-based MVCC: read at guarantee-ts T, a row is visible ⇔ insert ts ≤ T and no tombstone with ts ≤ T; the same data with a different T is a different snapshot, reads and writes never block.
  • How T is set: the consistency level (Strong/Bounded/Eventual) trades "how fresh vs how long to wait"; full mechanism in Lesson 30.

The index service

The first four parts nailed the write path: a row enters through the Proxy, is Appended to the WAL, accumulates into a growing segment, is flushed to binlogs, and is later merged tidy by compaction. But up to here, the vectors in those sealed segments are still just raw data — to ask "most like which?", you can only compute the distance from the query vector to every single stored vector (brute force). With many segments and many vectors, that scan becomes unacceptably slow. From here we enter Part 5, Indexing: first the index service — who decides which segments get an index, who actually builds it, and how the result becomes a "loadable" asset. The headline conclusion up front: index build is scheduled by DataCoord and executed by a worker (a datanode) — there is NO separate indexnode process (recall the "coordinators schedule, nodes execute" theme from Lessons 9 and 12).

📮 Analogy
Remember that library, "one bookshelf = a family of volumes"? The books are all shelved, but if a reader wants "books on vector search," must they walk the shelves one by one? Too slow. An efficient library builds a set of catalog cards (an index) for every shelf: sorted by topic and author, so a searcher flips a few cards and goes straight to the target. But the catalog doesn't grow itself. The head librarian (DataCoord) watches the whole library's ledger and decides "which shelves still lack a catalog, and what kind to build," then hands out tasks; the one who actually buries themselves in the shelf and writes cards is an assistant (a worker). The head librarian only schedules and registers, never copies cards; the assistant only does the work, then hands the cards back and registers them in the master catalog. Note: there is no third "dedicated card-copying department" — the card-copying is done on the side by the same assistant who usually tends the stacks. Grasp this and you have the skeleton of Milvus's index service.

Why an index service: from brute force to a loadable index

Lesson 5 (on ANN) planted the seed: an exact nearest-neighbor over high-dimensional vectors must compare against every datum, with cost growing linearly with size. A collection may hold hundreds of millions of vectors across hundreds or thousands of sealed segments; a per-row brute-force scan simply can't hold up in production. An index exists for exactly this: one offline preprocessing pass (build a graph, cluster, quantize…) organizes the data into a structure that lets a query skip most of it, compressing retrieval from "scan everything" to "look at a small candidate set." The price is that this preprocessing is itself heavy — read out a whole segment, run a complex algorithm, write the result back to storage. In other words, an index is a "pain now, gain later" bargain: spend extra effort once at build time, and every subsequent query reaps the benefit, amortizing that preprocessing cost across countless retrievals.

Precisely because building an index is heavy and slow, it can't sit in the write hot path (it would wreck write latency); it must be a background task, done asynchronously after data is sealed (immutable) — the very same philosophy as last lesson's compaction: decouple "fast bookkeeping" from "slow tidying." So we need an "index service" to answer three questions: which segments to build for (schedule), who builds (execute), and how the result is used (let QueryNode load it). Those three questions are this lesson's through-line.

It's worth making "just how heavy is an index build" concrete. Take the most common graph index, HNSW: to build a graph over hundreds of thousands or millions of vectors in a segment, the algorithm must repeatedly search each point's neighbors, link edges, and organize layers — work whose cost grows super-linearly with size. The IVF family must first cluster the whole segment's vectors (run k-means) and assign each vector to a bucket; quantized IVF_PQ additionally trains a codebook. All of this means a build eats lots of CPU/memory, reads out the whole segment, and produces sizeable index files. Putting such a heavy job in the write path would make every write wait for a "full-segment recompute," instantly destroying write latency. So Milvus's choice is a foregone conclusion — writes only land data into segments fast, and index building is left to the background, done slowly on immutable sealed segments. That is the plain yet hard engineering reason behind "an index must be a background service."

No index (brute force)

The query vector computes a distance against every single stored vector in a segment, cost O(N) growing linearly with size. More segments, more vectors, slower. Growing segments (not yet indexed) are queried exactly this way — it works, but only for small data.

With an index (ANN)

Vectors are pre-organized into a graph / inverted buckets / quantized codes; a query probes only a small candidate set, cost approaching O(log N) or sublinear. The cost is the heavy one-time build, so it runs in the background on sealed segments.

Who owns, who builds: DataCoord schedules + a worker executes

This is the point most easily misremembered, so nail it down: index build has no separate process called "indexnode." Its division of labor matches the main theme of Lesson 12 (DataCoord) and Lesson 17 (DataNode) exactly — coordinators schedule, nodes execute.

DataCoord is the brain: it owns the index metadata (which field of which collection has what index, and the state of each segment's index), with code in internal/datacoord/index_service.go (the outward RPCs such as CreateIndex), index_meta.go (metadata), and index_inspector.go (inspection: find "sealed segments not yet indexed" and emit build tasks). It does not read vectors or run algorithms itself.

The worker is the hands: the one that actually reads a segment's binlogs, calls Knowhere to run the build algorithm, and writes index files back to object storage is a worker — in today's architecture, that worker runs on a datanode (code in internal/datanode/index/task_index.go, the indexBuildTask). It implements the gRPC interface workerpb.IndexNodeClient — note: despite the "IndexNode" in the interface name, that is only a legacy protocol name and does NOT imply a separate indexnode process. This dovetails with Lesson 17: streaming writes went to StreamingNode, and the DataNode stepped back to do compaction and index builds.

Inside the worker, one build task is roughly three moves: read — pull the segment's vector binlogs out of object storage and assemble them into an in-memory dataset; build — hand that dataset to Knowhere with the index type and params and let it run the actual algorithm (build a graph / cluster / train a codebook); write — serialize Knowhere's output into index files and write them back to object storage, then report the paths to DataCoord. The worker is "just hands": it knows nothing about which segment "should" be built — that decision belongs entirely to DataCoord's inspector. This clean split of "the coordinator decides, the worker computes" is exactly what makes the whole thing scalable: add more workers and more segments build in parallel, with DataCoord still the single brain keeping the books.

DataCoord
owns index meta · inspects sealed segments · emits build tasks
→
build task queue
one task per sealed segment · dispatched to a worker
→
datanode worker
implements IndexNodeClient · reads binlogs · calls Knowhere · writes index files
→
write back meta
register index-file paths · mark segment "indexed"

The index lifecycle: CreateIndex → per-segment build task → index files → loadable

Stringing one build end to end is the best way to understand the index service. It is four steps, each mapping to a stage in the diagram above:

1

CreateIndex (declare intent)

index_service.go · CreateIndex

A user declares "build an index" on a field (with an index type and params). DataCoord records this index definition into metadata — a collection-level declaration that has not yet computed any segment.

2

Per-segment build task

index_inspector.go · createIndexForSegment

The inspector periodically scans: any segment that is sealed yet not built for this index gets one build task. As many sealed segments as a collection has, that many tasks go out (one per segment).

3

Worker builds index files

datanode/index · indexBuildTask

The worker takes a task: read the segment's vector binlogs → call Knowhere to build (graph / cluster / quantize) → write the resulting index files to object storage (recall Lesson 18's index_files prefix).

4

Register → loadable

The worker writes the index-file paths back to DataCoord's metadata and the segment is marked "indexed." The index is now a durable asset; when the collection is loaded, QueryNode can load it for ANN (next lesson, and Lesson 23).

One often-missed key in this lifecycle: CreateIndex is a "declaration," not "built instantly." After you call CreateIndex, the already-existing sealed segments are built segment by segment, asynchronously; and segments produced later (from continued writes and flushes) are auto-built with the same index by the inspector. So "building an index" is not a one-shot action but a continuously-in-effect policy: as long as the index definition exists, DataCoord ensures "every sealed segment eventually gets it." This also explains why interfaces like GetIndexState / DescribeIndex report progress ("built/building/total") — building an index is inherently a gradually-converging process.

Why build "per segment": granularity, failure, and retry

Notice a phrase that keeps recurring above: "one build task per sealed segment." The index service's scheduling granularity is not the collection, not the partition, but the segment. This is no arbitrary choice; it falls straight out of earlier parts: a segment is Milvus's smallest immutable, independently-processable unit of data — only a sealed segment (data frozen) is fit for indexing, because the build algorithm needs to see a fixed, unchanging dataset; a growing segment is still receiving new rows, so an index built on it would instantly go stale. Hence growing segments are simply not indexed and are queried by brute force (a point the next lesson and Lesson 23 re-emphasize).

Per-segment indexing brings three concrete benefits. First, parallelism: the build tasks for hundreds or thousands of segments are mutually independent and can be dispatched at once across many workers, spreading a large collection's "build" into many small concurrent tasks. Second, incrementality: writes keep producing new segments, and the inspector only needs to emit tasks for the newly-appeared, not-yet-built segments; already-built ones need no redo — the index "grows naturally" with the data. Third, failure isolation: if one segment's build task fails (a worker crashed, a binlog read errored), only that one segment is affected; DataCoord simply re-dispatches it, without touching other already-built segments.

Several sealed segments of one collection, each an independent build task: parallel, incremental, individually retriable on failure
seg #1indexed ✓
seg #2indexed ✓
seg #3building…
seg #4failed→retry
seg #5 (growing)no index · brute force

It is exactly this "per-segment task + state machine + retry" mechanism that lets the index service, in a distributed environment of continuous writes, crashing nodes, and flaky networks, steadily drive "every segment eventually gets indexed" to completion. This is also the root reason DataCoord must own the index metadata: it has to keep books for every segment — which step this segment reached, which index definition it uses, where its index files live, how many times it failed. Without this ledger, "gradual convergence" under distribution is impossible. Think of index_meta.go as that "index ledger," and index_inspector.go as the inspector who keeps reconciling and re-dispatches a task wherever a gap appears.

StageWhoOutput / state
CreateIndexProxy→DataCoordwrite the index definition to metadata (collection-level)
Inspect & emit tasksDataCoord (inspector)each not-yet-built sealed segment → one build task
Builddatanode workerread binlogs · call Knowhere · write index files to object storage
Registerworker→DataCoordindex-file paths into metadata · mark segment "indexed"
LoadQueryNodeon collection load, read the index into memory / mmap for ANN

On auto-index: many users don't want to agonize over "HNSW or IVF, and how to tune the params." Milvus offers AUTOINDEX — you merely declare "build an index on this field," and the system auto-picks a reasonable index type and params by field type and data scale. Its essence still runs the exact same chain: CreateIndex declares → inspector emits tasks → worker builds with Knowhere → register as loadable; only the "which index, what params" step shifts from user-specified to system-chosen. For beginners, AUTOINDEX is the most worry-free default; switch to a concrete type when you want to fine-tune (next lesson dissects each index family).

Let's also connect ahead how this "index service" relates to the next two lessons, so you don't memorize them in isolation. This lesson is about "how the act of building an index is scheduled and executed" — a control-flow view. The next lesson (22) drills into the Knowhere library that the worker calls, seeing exactly which vector-index families exist (FLAT/IVF/HNSW/DiskANN…), their tradeoffs and params. The lesson after (23) follows the data flow, walking the physical path "sealed segment's raw vectors → worker builds → index files to object storage → QueryNode loads into memory or mmap." The three are three facets of one thing: who schedules (this lesson), with what algorithm (Lesson 22), how data flows (Lesson 23). Stack them together and you truly understand Milvus indexing.

Finally, a design observation worth savoring: the index service almost exactly mirrors compaction's paradigm — both are scheduled by DataCoord, both execute on a datanode worker, both act only on immutable sealed segments, both write results to object storage and then atomically register them into metadata, and both are background tasks that don't disturb the write hot path. This is no coincidence but the same background-maintenance skeleton Milvus reuses: peel the "slow and heavy, but makes reads faster" work off the foreground and let the coordinator amortize it when the system is idle. Once you understand compaction, you understand the index service almost for free; and once you understand this generic skeleton, you hold the key to Milvus's entire "after the write" world.

internal/datacoord/index_service.goCreateIndex: declare the index definition, accepted by DataCoord (excerpt)
// DataCoord's outward RPC: accept the "build an index on a field" declaration, write it to index meta.
// Note: this only "registers intent"; the per-segment build is dispatched asynchronously by the inspector to a worker.

    // health check, param validation, allocate indexID...

🌍 Big picture
In one line: vectors in a sealed segment, if unindexed, can only be brute-force scanned row by row (slow). The index service makes "building an index" a background task — DataCoord schedules (index_service.go/index_meta.go/index_inspector.go: owns metadata, inspects sealed segments, emits one build task per segment), and a datanode worker executes (indexBuildTask, implementing workerpb.IndexNodeClient, with no separate indexnode process: read binlogs, call Knowhere, write index files, write back meta). CreateIndex is a declaration, not an instant build; new segments are auto-built; AUTOINDEX lets the system pick the type and params.
📌 Key points
  • Why index: without one, you can only brute-force O(N) per-row distances; an index trades a heavy offline preprocess for "look at a small candidate set" at query time.
  • Schedule vs execute: DataCoord owns index metadata and schedules (CreateIndex in index_service.go, index_inspector.go); a datanode worker executes (indexBuildTask).
  • No indexnode: the worker implements workerpb.IndexNodeClient, but that's just a protocol name — the build runs on a datanode; there is no separate indexnode process (echoing Lessons 9 and 17).
  • Lifecycle: CreateIndex (declare) → one build task per sealed segment → worker reads binlogs · calls Knowhere · writes index files → registered, then loadable by QueryNode.
  • Declare, not instant: existing segments build asynchronously one by one, new sealed segments auto-build; progress is queryable via GetIndexState/DescribeIndex.
  • AUTOINDEX: the system auto-picks index type and params by field type and scale; the chain is unchanged, only the type-selection is automated.

  4. RootCoord builds all indexes

Knowhere vector indexes

Last lesson nailed "who builds the index": DataCoord schedules, a datanode worker executes. But in that step the worker merely called a library — the one that truly knows "how to organize vectors into a fast-searchable structure" is Milvus's upstream vector-search kernel, Knowhere. This lesson drills into Knowhere to see which vector-index families it offers (FLAT, the IVF line, HNSW, DiskANN, SCANN, GPU, sparse), what each is about, its key params, and when to pick which. One important caveat up front: most index names below (HNSW, IVF_SQ8, SCANN, CAGRA…) are IndexEnum constants in the Knowhere library, not defined in Milvus's own code; only a few enums are referenced in Milvus core, and this lesson distinguishes them precisely.

📮 Analogy
Picture running a warehouse packed with goods, your job being "given a sample, find the few items most like it." You have several ways to organize the shelves: The dumbest but most accurate is to compare every item against the sample (that's FLAT, brute force, 100% recall but slow); a bit smarter, first zone the goods by similarity (cluster), locate the sample to the nearest few zones and only browse those (that's IVF: coarse buckets first, fine search within); smarter still, string a net of "neighbor shortcuts" between items so that from any item you hop a few shortcuts to the patch most like the sample (that's HNSW: a navigable small-world graph); to save space, compress each item into a thumbnail before comparing (that's PQ quantization: approximation for memory); when it won't fit in memory, spread the shelves onto disk and keep only the index skeleton in memory (that's DiskANN). There is no "best" method, only "how you choose to trade off among speed, memory, recall, and cost." Knowhere is the toolbox that stocks all of these methods.

What is Knowhere: Milvus's vector-search kernel

Knowhere is a standalone, upstream vector-search library maintained by the Milvus project (primarily C++, wrapping FAISS, HNSWlib, DiskANN, cuVS, etc. behind a unified API). Milvus's C++ core does its two most core algorithmic jobs — building indexes and vector search — by calling Knowhere; think of it as Milvus's "vector compute engine." Recall Lesson 2's "three-language split": Go handles distribution and scheduling, C++ handles single-machine storage and compute, Rust handles full-text indexing; and within that C++ layer, the concrete algorithms of vector indexing and search are outsourced to this dedicated library, Knowhere.

Why factor the algorithms into a separate library instead of intermixing them with Milvus core? Because vector search is a fast-evolving yet highly specialized field: HNSW is hot today, DiskANN tomorrow, GPU CAGRA the day after, with FAISS, cuVS and a dozen other implementations underneath, each with its own strengths. Encapsulating those algorithm details inside Knowhere lets it focus on "how to build and search vectors fast and accurately," while Milvus core only says, through a unified interface, "use index X with metric Y, build/search"; the two evolve independently. That is the value of clean layering: Milvus need not touch distributed scheduling to add a new index, and Knowhere need not know about segments, the WAL, or replicas. Every index family this lesson covers is part of the "capability menu" Knowhere exposes to Milvus.

This yields a fact important for reading code, and the citation discipline this lesson keeps stressing: because the index algorithms live in Knowhere (the upstream library), most index-type names are Knowhere IndexEnum constants, not symbols in Milvus core. In the Milvus repo, what you can see are the spots where core code references these Knowhere enums — e.g. internal/core/src/index/Utils.cpp references knowhere::IndexEnum::INDEX_FAISS_IVFFLAT, INDEX_SPARSE_INVERTED_INDEX, INDEX_SPARSE_WAND, and internal/core/src/common/Utils.h references INDEX_DISKANN. But names like HNSW, IVF_SQ8, IVF_PQ, SCANN, GPU_CAGRA — do not hunt for their "definition" in Milvus core; they are defined in the Knowhere upstream library. When this lesson mentions these names, it treats them strictly as "Knowhere index names," never forcing them onto some Milvus file that lacks them.

The vector-index families: idea, params, when to use

The table below is this lesson's "map." Read it well and you grasp the backbone of "facing a field, how to choose an index":

Family (Knowhere name)Core ideaKey paramsWhen to pick it
FLATbrute force, per-row distancenonesmall data, want 100% recall, baseline comparison
IVF_FLATcluster into buckets, query probes only the nearest fewnlist (buckets) / nprobe (probed)medium scale, want recall-speed balance, no precision loss
IVF_SQ8IVF + scalar quantization (1 byte per dim)nlist / nprobememory-tight, can accept slight precision loss
IVF_PQIVF + product quantization (short codes)nlist / nprobe / m / nbitshuge scale, very tight memory, tolerate more precision loss
HNSWnavigable small-world graph, hop along neighbor edgesM (edges/node) / efConstruction / ef (query)want high recall + low latency, memory sufficient (most common)
DiskANNon-disk graph index, only skeleton in memorysearch_list / beamwidth, etc.data far exceeds memory, trade SSD for capacity
SCANNanisotropic quantization + reorder, Google-derivednlist / nprobe / reorder_kwant higher recall on a quantized index
GPU (CAGRA / GPU_IVF_*)graph / IVF on GPU, massive parallelismCPU-equivalents + GPU resourceshave a GPU, need very high throughput
Sparse (SPARSE_INVERTED_INDEX / SPARSE_WAND)inverted index for sparse vectors + WAND pruningdrop_ratio, etc.sparse vectors (e.g. BM25 / SPLADE learned-sparse)

Reading the table, two through-lines suffice. Line one is "bucketing vs graph": the IVF line takes the "cluster first, query looks at only the nearest few buckets" route, its core params being nlist (how many buckets) and nprobe (how many to probe) — bigger nprobe means more buckets seen, higher recall but slower; HNSW takes the "build a net of neighbor shortcuts and hop edge-by-edge to the target patch" route, its core params being M (edges per point — denser graph, more accurate but more memory) and ef (candidate-set size at query time — larger means higher recall but slower). Line two is "raw vs quantized": FLAT/IVF_FLAT/HNSW store raw vectors (accurate but memory-hungry), while SQ8/PQ compress them (memory-saving but precision-losing). Cross these two lines and you understand why the IVF_FLAT, IVF_SQ8, IVF_PQ series exists — all "the IVF bucketing skeleton" plus "different compression strengths."

It helps to give each family a one-line "personality." FLAT is the honest plodder: it never approximates, so its recall is always 100% — invaluable as a ground-truth baseline when you want to measure how much recall another index actually loses, but hopeless at scale. IVF is the zoning planner: cluster once, then at query time visit only the nearest zones; simple, predictable, and its accuracy is tuned almost entirely by nprobe. HNSW is the well-connected navigator: by building a multi-layer shortcut graph it reaches the right neighborhood in just a few hops, delivering the best latency-recall combo most workloads want — at the price of holding the whole graph (and raw vectors) in memory. DiskANN is the warehouse-on-disk: when even quantization can't squeeze the data into RAM, it puts the graph on SSD and keeps only a navigating skeleton in memory, trading a bit of latency for the ability to serve datasets far larger than memory. The quantized IVFs (SQ8/PQ) are the space-savers: SQ8 squashes each dimension to a byte, PQ chops a vector into sub-vectors and replaces each with a codebook entry, shrinking footprint dramatically while accepting some recall loss that a re-rank step can partly recover.

"I want max recall & low latency, RAM is fine"

Reach for HNSW. The graph gives near-exhaustive recall at a few-hop cost; tune ef up if recall must be higher. This is the default sweet spot for most production vector search.

"My data won't fit in RAM"

Quantize with IVF_PQ/SQ8 to shrink it, or go DiskANN to put the bulk on SSD. You trade a little recall (quantization) or a little latency (disk) for the capacity to serve far more vectors per node.

A quick word on sparse vectors, since they look different from everything above. Dense embeddings have a few hundred always-present dimensions; sparse vectors (from BM25 or learned-sparse models like SPLADE) have a huge dimensionality but only a handful of non-zero entries per vector — essentially "term → weight" maps. They can't be bucketed or graphed the same way, so Knowhere serves them with an inverted index (SPARSE_INVERTED_INDEX): for each dimension, a posting list of "which vectors have it and with what weight." The WAND variant (SPARSE_WAND) adds dynamic pruning — it computes upper-bound scores and skips candidates that cannot possibly enter the topK, much like text search engines do. These are the two sparse-vector enums that genuinely appear in Milvus core's index/Utils.cpp.

Where it computes: memory, disk, GPU

Different families also assume different storage media, which directly sets capacity ceilings and cost. Lay them out by the "compute/storage hierarchy":

GPU VRAMGPU indexes (CAGRA / GPU_IVF_*)massive parallelism, very high throughput; bounded by VRAM, needs a GPU card
memoryHNSW / IVF_FLAT / IVF_SQ8 / IVF_PQ / FLATindex resident in memory or mmap; most common, low latency; capacity bounded by RAM (quantization compresses)
disk(SSD)DiskANNgraph body on SSD, only skeleton in memory; trade disk capacity to "fit huge data," latency slightly up
sparseSPARSE_INVERTED_INDEX / SPARSE_WANDinverted structures for sparse vectors, with WAND dynamic pruning to skip low-score candidates

The core message of this hierarchy is a triangle tradeoff: speed, memory (cost), recall — you can usually prioritize only two. HNSW maxes out "speed + recall" at the cost of memory; IVF_PQ/SQ8 save memory via quantization at the cost of recall; DiskANN trades disk for capacity at the cost of slightly higher latency; GPU trades hardware for throughput at the cost of GPU expense. No index wins all three corners at once — choosing an index is, in essence, picking a landing point in this triangle per your data scale, latency target, memory budget, and recall floor. This echoes the refrain from Lesson 5 on ANN: approximate search is an engineering tradeoff among "fast enough, cheap enough, accurate enough."

How one IVF search skips most of the data

Making "bucket pruning" concrete best conveys why an index is fast. Take IVF: suppose the build clustered the segment's vectors into 4 buckets (nlist=4) and the query sets nprobe=2:

1

Locate nearest buckets

The query vector q first computes distance to the 4 buckets' centroids and picks the nearest nprobe=2. The other 2 buckets are skipped entirely, never looked at.

2

In-bucket exact compute

Only within the 2 chosen buckets, compute distance per vector (IVF_FLAT computes exactly on raw vectors; SQ8/PQ approximate via compressed codes).

3

Merge for topK

Merge and sort the candidates from those 2 buckets and take the nearest topK. Scanned volume drops from "the whole segment" to "about half" — smaller nprobe drops it more and runs faster, but risks missing a bucket (lower recall).

This flow demonstrates the nprobe tradeoff plainly: raise nprobe → more buckets seen, lower chance of missing a true neighbor, recall up, but more vectors to compute, slower; lower nprobe is the reverse. HNSW's ef is the "graph version" of the same dial: bigger ef → larger candidate set maintained at query time → more thorough search → higher recall but slower. Remember this general rule: nearly every ANN index has such a "search a bit more ↔ a bit higher recall, but slower" runtime dial; turning it slides between speed and recall. Build-time params (nlist, M) set "the skeleton and its ceiling"; query-time params (nprobe, ef) adjust "the tightness of this particular search" on that skeleton. As an aside: HNSW search is really the "graph version" of the same idea — starting from the graph's top entry, each step greedily hops toward a neighbor closer to q, rapidly approaching the rough region on the sparse upper layers, then descending layer by layer into the dense bottom for a fine search, finally converging candidates onto the target neighborhood. Whether bucketing or graph, the essence is the same: "exact-compute a tiny subset, skip most overall."

An iron rule: the metric must match the field

Finally, stress a point that is easy to trip on yet severe: the index's metric must match the metric declared for the field at schema time. Recall Lesson 4 — a vector's "similarity" is defined by a metric: L2 (Euclidean, smaller is closer), IP (inner product, larger is closer), COSINE (cosine, more aligned direction is closer). If a field's embeddings were trained under COSINE semantics but you build an L2 index on them, then what the index "thinks is near" and what you "actually want as near" disagree, and results are systematically wrong — with no error raised, just inexplicably poor recall. So when Knowhere builds an index, the metric is a first-class parameter bound to the index and must align across the field, the index, and the metric you used to train the embeddings. This is one of the most insidious bug classes in vector search; get it right from the very start. Milvus does validate metric-vs-field consistency at build time, but using the semantically right metric is ultimately yours to guard.

To tidy up "params," the clearest way is to split them by two moments. Build-time params (IVF's nlist, HNSW's M and efConstruction, PQ's m/nbits) decide the index structure itself — how many buckets, how dense the graph, how big the codebook; once built they are frozen into the index files and changing them means a rebuild. Query-time params (nprobe, ef) are dials you can adjust per search without rebuilding, sliding the current query along "speed ↔ recall." Grasping this "build sets the skeleton, query tunes the tightness" split keeps the two kinds apart when tuning: if recall is chronically too low, first suspect a too-small structural param like nlist/M (needs a rebuild); if you just want more recall on a particular query, bump nprobe/ef (no rebuild).

Finally, connect this lesson back to the main line. Here we covered what the Knowhere algorithm kernel offers and how to choose; but which segment's data these indexes are built on, where the built files go, and how QueryNode puts them to use are matters of data flow — exactly the "sealed segment → build → index files to object storage → QueryNode loads" journey of the next lesson (23). Combine "which index to choose" (this lesson) with "how the index flows" (next lesson) and you truly command Milvus's vector indexing.

internal/core/src/index/Utils.cppMilvus core references Knowhere's index enums (illustrative excerpt)
// These are IndexEnum constants from the upstream Knowhere library; Milvus core references them here:
knowhere::IndexEnum::INDEX_FAISS_IVFFLAT      // IVF_FLAT
knowhere::IndexEnum::INDEX_FAISS_BIN_IVFFLAT  // binary-vector IVF
knowhere::IndexEnum::INDEX_SPARSE_INVERTED_INDEX // sparse inverted
knowhere::IndexEnum::INDEX_SPARSE_WAND        // sparse WAND pruning
// See also common/Utils.h referencing knowhere::IndexEnum::INDEX_DISKANN (DiskANN).
// Whereas HNSW / IVF_SQ8 / IVF_PQ / SCANN / GPU_CAGRA are defined inside Knowhere; core has no definition of them.
🌍 Big picture
In one line: the algorithm kernel the worker calls to build/search is the upstream library Knowhere. It stocks a range of vector-index families — FLAT (brute force · full recall), IVF_FLAT/SQ8/PQ (cluster-bucket + optional quantization, dials nlist/nprobe), HNSW (neighbor graph, dials M/ef), DiskANN (on-disk graph for capacity), SCANN, GPU/CAGRA (hardware for throughput), sparse (SPARSE_INVERTED_INDEX/SPARSE_WAND). Selection is a speed↔memory↔recall triangle; the metric must match the field. Citation discipline: most index names are Knowhere IndexEnum, and Milvus core only references a few (the IVF/sparse ones in index/Utils.cpp, INDEX_DISKANN in common/Utils.h).
📌 Key points
  • Knowhere = vector kernel: Milvus C++ core does index build and vector search by calling the upstream Knowhere library (wraps FAISS/HNSWlib/DiskANN/cuVS).
  • Index families: FLAT (full recall, slow) / IVF_FLAT·SQ8·PQ (bucketing ± quantization) / HNSW (neighbor graph, most common) / DiskANN (disk) / SCANN / GPU·CAGRA / sparse.
  • Two through-lines: bucketing (IVF: nlist/nprobe) vs graph (HNSW: M/ef); raw (accurate · memory) vs quantized (memory-saving · precision loss).
  • Triangle tradeoff: speed ↔ memory (cost) ↔ recall, usually only two prioritized; selection is picking a point in the triangle.
  • Runtime dial: larger nprobe/ef → higher recall but slower; build-time params (nlist/M) set the skeleton, query-time params (nprobe/ef) set the tightness.
  • Metric must match: L2/IP/COSINE must align with the field and with embedding training, else results are systematically wrong with no error.
  • Citation discipline: most index names are Knowhere IndexEnum; core only references a few (index/Utils.cpp, INDEX_DISKANN in common/Utils.h).

🧪 Self-test - think about the design

1. Where do Milvus's vector indexes (HNSW, IVF_PQ, SCANN, GPU CAGRA, etc.) actually come from?
  1. Each index type is a standalone microservice
  2. Generated on the fly by the Proxy at query time
  3. Most are IndexEnum types provided by the upstream Knowhere library, not defined by milvus core itself; core only references a few constants (e.g. INDEX_DISKANN in common/Utils.h, INDEX_FAISS_IVFFLAT/INDEX_SPARSE_INVERTED_INDEX/INDEX_SPARSE_WAND etc. as knowhere::IndexEnum:: in index/Utils.cpp)
  4. All implemented by milvus core's Go code
Show answer & explanation click to expand
Answer: C. Vector index algorithms belong to Knowhere (wrapping Faiss/HNSWlib/DiskANN/cuVS, etc.). When citing a milvus file you may only cite IndexEnum constants that truly exist; the rest should be presented as 'Knowhere index names,' not forced into milvus files that lack them
2. When choosing a vector index and its params, which statement is correct?
  1. HNSW needs no parameters at all
  2. The metric can be swapped freely at query time without affecting results
  3. The smaller nprobe is, the higher the recall
  4. IVF's nlist/nprobe and HNSW's M/ef are all 'recall vs speed/memory' knobs; FLAT brute force gives 100% recall but is slowest, PQ/SQ8 trade accuracy for memory via compression, DiskANN puts the graph on disk for huge scale; and the query metric must match the one used to build the index
Show answer & explanation click to expand
Answer: D. Every ANN index trades within the 'recall–latency–memory' triangle; raising nprobe/ef lifts recall but is slower. The metric (L2/IP/COSINE, etc.) must match what the field was indexed with, otherwise distance semantics break and results are meaningless
